===== PAGE 5 =====
CENTER
YEAR BOOK
1961 -1962
5722
Jacksonville Jewish Center
Year Book Theme
The theme of our cover is in tribute to the activities of our lay leadership this past year. We have used the Torah which indicates
the heritage which was assumed by all of Israel.
The question is often asked, "Who are they who must fulfill the responsibilities of the Torah?"
The answer that is often given places the responsibility upon the Rabbis and the scholars for the preservation of Jewish life. The
Jacksonville Jewish Center during the past year gave another answer to this question. They adhered to Jewish tradition by showing
that in the absence of rabbinic leadership the obligation of upholdi ng the Torah rests upon the shoulders of each and every layman.
In the other illustrations we have depicted the various types of activities in which our laymen have participated.
With this issue of the Year Book we take note of the importance of lay leadership and lay participation in our congregation. In
the synagogue, in the classroom, in the choir or in committees, our laymen have proven how vital they are to the success of our program.
"How goodly are your tents, O Jacob, your dwelling places, O Israel

David Weinstein
Administrator
We of the Administration staff would like to take this oppor¬
tunity of extending to Rabbi Goldblum our sincere welcome. In the
short time since the Rabbi has assumed the position, his warm per¬
sonality and sincere guidance have given us greater impetus in the
administrative work of the Center.
We would like to take this opportunity of extending to the offi¬
cers and lay leaders of the congregation and our fellow staff mem¬
bers best wishes for a Happy and Prosperous New Year.
As one of the outstanding Synagogue-Centers in the Southeast,
we offer to our community a unique program covering every phase
of religious, educational, social, cultural and athletic programming.
In brief summary we are outlining our principal activities which
are described in pictures and words throughout our entire Year Book.
Maintenance Staff
SCOPE OF ACTIVITIES
Religious Services—Traditional prayer services are held daily,
morning and evening in the Chapel.
Friday Family Services, Sabbath and Holiday Services are
observed in the Sanctuary.
Personal Religious Observances—Traditional Jewish customs
are observed in connection with Circumcision, naming of
girls, Pidyon Ha-ben, Bar Mitzvah, Bas Mitzvah, Confir¬
mations, Marriages, Conversion to Judaism, Bereavement.
Education—Daily Kindergarten with outstanding program of
secular and religious content.
Hebrew School, carefully graded with intensive Hebrew
content.
Sunday School, with a curriculum including Jewish history
and customs.
Supplementary religious educational training is practiced in
Pre-Jr., Jr. Congregation, youth services and Minyan Club.
Extension Services
Library—outstanding collection of juvenile and adult
Jewish books.
Adult Education—courses on religious and Jewish cul¬
tural subjects.
Music—a program of religious and cultural Jewish
music.
Sisterhood and Men’s Club render valuable service to the
Center.
Social Activities provided by Mr. and Mrs. Club, Amity
Club and "32 Club.”
Youth Activities
United Synagogue Youth. Various age levels are served
by Center Youth League, Charles Palot Club, Major’s Pre-
Teens, Tween-Agers.
Scouting.
Athletics—programs for youth and adults.
Community Service.

===== PAGE 15 =====
Dedication of To field Memorial Library
On Sunday, May 28, as the highlight of the United Synagogue
Southeast Region Conference, the Jacksonville Jewish Center dedi¬
cated its new Judaica library in memory of our late departed and
beloved Rabbi, Dr. Sanders A. Tofield.
The solemn and inspiring memorial service, chaired by Mr.
Harry Gendzier, was addressed by Dr. Isaac Klein of Buffalo’s
Temple Emanuel, a lifelong friend of Rabbi Tofield of sainted
memory. Also participating in the services were Rabbis Morris B.
Chapman, Moshe Goldblum, and Allen Rutchik.
President Louis Safer presented a facsimile of the dedication
and memorial plaque to Mrs. Sanders A. Tofield. Mr. Joe Zel,
chairman of the Library Committee, presented the library to the
Center.
The library, made possible by contributions of members and
friends of the Center, has as its nucleus, the personal library of our
late Rabbi. Over 1500 volumes in Hebrew and English covering a
wide variety of subjects of Jewish content for adults and school
children are available for reference, study, and pleasure reading.
Rare old additions as well as the latest best sellers are in circulation
and new books are constantly being added. Our library is reputed
to be one of the largest of its kind in this part of the country. In
charge of organizing the library was our Library Committee, aug¬
mented by voluntary help from numerous members as well as our
young people, and spearheaded by Mrs. Philip Selber, Mrs. Bernard
Harry Gendzier, Lionel Games, Mrs. Philip Selber and
Joe Zel in our Library.
Glossman, and Miss Minnie Schrieber. Plans are being made to
have the library open several days each week for the convenience of
our members.
We look forward to a year of continued expansion and service
to the congregation.
Dedication of the Library
Mrs. Tofield holding the Library plaque

===== PAGE 17 =====
That Count
Nine Rabbis do not constitute a minyan, but ten laymen do.
This lesson was taught to us very tragically during this past year.
With the passing of Rabbi Tofield, of blessed memory, our con¬
gregation was left without Rabbinic leadership. With the lay
leadership which the Jacksonville Jewish Center possesses, the
program continued in all of its fullness and significance. Few
congregations can take pride in such devotion, self sacrifice of
time and effort for the cause of Jewish life.
The moral of this past year’s experience is not that we now
have a Rabbi and it is our turn to relax. The moral lies rather in
our appreciating how important we all are to the success of our
synagogue program. A Rabbi without the full cooperation of the
membership is indeed without success, a congregation without
Rabbinic guidance can still accomplish much. It is necessary that
we realize our own personal obligation to the Center program
and recognize the important role that we can play in making that
program vital and meaningful.
(simulated Havdalah Service)
Your Officers and Board have performed superhuman tasks
in maintaining the high level of activity during this past year.
But they need your cooperation if the Center is to move forward.
They need your presence at religious services, your advice and
assistance on committees, and your efforts in maintaining these
fine facilities which make our program possible.
To their plea for help I add my own. May the blessings of
health and happiness be yours for the coming year as you bless
us with your constant presence and assistance.
May you be inscribed in the book of meaningful life for the
coming year. "Le Shonah Tovah Tikatevu.”

===== PAGE 19 =====
President's Message
An outstanding highlight of Center history this
past year has been the traditional concept of partici¬
pation by Klal Yisroel (laymen) in synagogue activi¬
ties. The zeal and diligence of our officers, commit¬
tee chairmen and laymen have continued to improve
the religious life, educational program and youth ac¬
tivities of our Center. To all of these people and to
our loyal staff I wish to extend my sincere thanks for
their invaluable aid in the administration of our insti¬
tution.
It was with great pleasure that we announced that
the position of Rabbi in our congregation was assumed
by Rabbi Moshe V. Goldblum. I take this means to wel¬
come officially Rabbi and Mrs. Goldblum and their
children, Simeon, Charles, David, Liba and Ora to our
midst. Later in the year, we plan to have an outstand¬
ing installation affair for Rabbi Goldblum. In the short
time he has been here, his warm sincerity and genuine
devotion have imbued those with whom he has come
into contact with a new spirit of understanding and
Head table at the Banquet of United Synagogue Convention held
in Jacksonville under chairmanship of Louis Safer, Vice-Pres.
of region.
Left to right: Mr. Louis Safer, Mrs. Safer, Rabbi Moshe V.
Goldblum, Mrs. Goldblum, Rabbi Allen Rutchik, Rabbi Irving
Lehrman
LOUIS SAFER, President
dedication to our purpose. The officers and lav lead¬
ers of the congregation look forward to a year of ex¬
panded participation in all phases of synagogue activi¬
ty under Rabbi Goldblum’s inspired spiritual guidance.
On behalf of the Officers, Directors and Trustees,
I extend to our entire Center Family, my best wishes
for a year of good health, spiritual enrichment, pros¬
perity and peace.
L’Shan oh Toveh Tikatevu

===== PAGE 31 =====
Building Fund-1962
The generous Building Fund contributions made by the Center Family over
the past years have provided us with one of the finest physical plants in the South¬
east Region, whereby we can carry on our progressive educational, religious, social
and youth activities programs. The continued support of our Center Family is
needed in order to meet the Building Fund needs for 1962 which are as follows:
1. Closing deficit for 1962 approximately $23,800.00
2. Mortgage payments 24,900.00
3. Building renovations 5,000.00
Robert J. Gordon, Chairman
In order to maintain properly the building, a minimum
of $5,000.00 is set aside each year to provide for major
building renovations and redecorations which the ordinary
maintenance budget does not permit. For example, in 1961
thisamount was used to reinsulate all of the air conditioning
handling equipment and to place the roof and all parapet
walls in good condition. In 1962 this amount will be used
to paint both the exterior and interior of the Activities Build¬
ing except for the Auditorium.
4.Purchase of Air Conditioning
Chilling Equipment 22,500.00
The air handling units throughout the build¬
ings are now in good condition, but mechanical
chilling equipment being 11 years old, is in poor
condition and requires excessive maintenance.
More important, however, is the fact that we
have a capacity of only 80 tons instead of the
minimum of 200 tons actually needed. There
was included in last years Building Fund budget
one-half of the total cost of putting in the needed
chilling equipment. We must raise the other
half.
Total 1962 Needs 76,200.00
Available from other sources:
Year Book $6,000.00
Air Conditioning Fund $6,000.00 12,000.00
1962 Pledges Needed $64,200.00
The Auditorium being used at the High Holidays for services.

Center Sisterhood
President Mrs. William Rose

MRS. WM. ROSE
With the advent of the New Year, our Sisterhood will begin
more than 40 years of service to local Conservative Judaism. We
have just completed a successful year in which we raised funds
with our Donor to finish furnishing our school building. We
were also able to fulfill our obligation to the Center toward pay¬
ment of Sunday School teachers and assisting with the Youth
program.
The coming year brings new obligations. Our funds are de¬
rived largely from dues and any fund raising activities that we
might hold. This year we hope to have a fall dance and a gala
Purim affair. We are also continuing the sale of Barton’s candy.
It is your support with the purchase of tickets and voluntary help
in planning that makes for our success.
Speaking for the officers of the Sisterhood, may we wish you
a very happy and prosperous New Year. We hope we will see
you at all Sisterhood functions, so that together we may continue
to help the growth and progress of our synagogue.

A SISTERHOOD TRIBUTE
by One of Our Devoted Sisterhood Members
The Center was built in ’27,
At 3rd & Silver ... a new bit of heaven.
On Rabbi Benjamin, the founder, we could dote,
And Cantor Edgar sang in a sweet note.
The good ladies promised all their support,
They worked very hard, and were always 'dort.’
But even tho promises often flee like the bird,
These were the ladies that kept their word.
They organized dances & rummage sales,
To pay our expenses, they made money in bales.
They even made a party on the block,
And saved all the profits down deep in their sock.
A new auxiliary was formed,
Daughters of Israel was its name.
It was later changed to Sisterhood,
But the purpose always remained the same.
The mortgage was big,
The income was small.
So they really had to dig.
To get us out of the hole.
It was a great struggle
And for the men’s help we yearned.
But the debts were paid off,
And the mortgage was burned.
As time went on, the auditorium got too small,
A new building was built . . . with a social hall,
Did we forget the youth . . . not at all,
We even built them a place for basketball.
Of its beautification, Sisterhood took great care,
With its many projects, it did more than its share.
They ran a bazaar, a luncheon and a Donor,
They raised enough money, to furnish from corner to corner.
They decorated the pulpit . . .
’Tis a beautiful sight
And they place flowers upon it
On Holidays and Friday night.
After services, in the auditorium they meet,
And with a "Good Shabus,” they one another greet,
The children come in too, for the bounteous fare,
Sisterhood just seems to be everywhere.
They stock the library with all sorts of books of knowledge
Where our boys and girls do some studying for college.
They give gifts and prizes for confirmation
And always remember the graduation.
In summertime the day camp is very much fun
In Jewish living and learning — much is done
Sisterhood funds then send them to a convention,
Where they learn about Israel more than we can mention.
For each holiday, the Sisterhood is there . . .
Goodies for the children to prepare
Chanukah they provide them with dredlech
They play games — the boys and the medlach
On Chamishoh Osor B’Shvat they give them to boot
Products of the Holy Land — mostly fruit
And each child is privileged for a small fee
To plant in Israel, a beautiful tree.
Purim with groggers they make much noise
The wonderful combination of medlach and boys
They sing a solo:
"Yarzeit Nach Haman’s Mapoloh”
Then follows the big celebration
Queen Esther is chosen at the coronation
And soon comes Pesach with the Seders
With the traditional foods that Sisterhood caters
On Shevous all the children are invited
To attend the solemn confirmation
Then with punch and cookies they are delighted
Followed by wishes for an enjoyable vacation
To the closing luncheon it is decided
That the Center president should be invited
They present him with a green paper in the Center s honor
With a "1” and three zeros in each corner.
Succoth the men build a Succah fine.
And for Kiddush the ladies bring wine.
They decorate the Succah with flowers and fruit
God bless the hands of Sisterhood.
Even on Simchas Torah, Sisterhoods work does not lag—
They encourage the Children to follow the Torah with a flag.
By Mrs. Rose Sloat
37

Milton Felder
The Center Men’s Club is looking forward to its 17th year of
existence with renewed dedication.
Looking over the achievements of last year we find the Men’s
Club accomplished the following: three scholarships for Camp
Ramah, flags for Simhat Torah Hakofot services; paid-up member¬
ship dinner, Blood Bank drive, adult Jewish education, Sunday Re¬
ligious School picnic, Minyan breakfasts, monthly meetings and
dinners.
The Center Men’s Club has shown itself to be a very vital part
of our Jewish Community and we are striving to widen and improve
our services to you and your children.
The new administration which includes officers, board of direc¬
tors and executive committee composed of men who have been out¬
standing in their zeal for the organization is ready to initiate many
new activities while continuing with traditional programs. At our
monthly dinner meetings we will again serve delicious meals and
present programs on religion, sports and politics. Dinner meetings
are held the first Thursday of the month.
All men interested in affiliation may contact one of the officers
or come to visit us at regularly scheduled dinner meetings.

Our Center Family this past year felt the loss of our beloved Rabbi Tofield of blessed
memory under whose leadership we have made steady progress for the last decade and a
half. Faced with the loss of a spiritual leader, for so many months, a less dedicated congrega¬
tion might have found it impossible to carry on its program.
However, our congregation met the situation with increased dedication and a firm deter¬
mination to maintain the status quo until we could once more enjoy able leadership. We
are greatly indebted to our devoted lay leaders and loyal staff members who willingly shoul¬
dered extra responsibilities during these crucial months, in order to maintain the dignity and
the continuity of our Sabbath and Holiday Services and other religious functions.
Nathan Freidlin, Chairman
We are especially grateful to our President, Mr. Louis Safter, and
other Board and staff members who on many occasions actually con¬
ducted services, read the Torah, coached our Bnai Mitzvah, prepared
interesting and meaningful explanations of the Torah and Haftorah
readings each Sabbath and participated in numerous other ways.
We also wish to acknowledge with thanks the ready and warm
response of our neighboring congregations and of the United Syna-
Our congregation is again blessed with a worthy Rabbi at our
helm. We feel honored that Rabbi Moshe Goldblum accepted our
gogue of America whenever we called upon them for Rabbinical
assistance.
pulpit, and we feel confident that under his leadership and with
God’s help, we shall once more go forward from strength to strength.
A simulated Synagogue scene at Sukkot
"Toil not merely for worldly goods. Find time also for the study of Torah ”

===== PAGE 69 =====
Nathan Schneider
Religious Education
We are pleased to report the following progress in
our Religious Education Department:
Hebrew School Enrollment — 158 boys, 78 girls,
total 236.
One of the main goals of this department has been
accomplished in the implementation of an intensive
and extensive graded school. With an expanded full
time staff we have been able to increase the number of
classes which now serve pupils ranging from 7 yrs. to
Post Confirmation. One of the major innovations is
our supper program which presents an opportunity of
acquainting our pupils with an additional facet of
Jewish ritual and ceremony.
Ted Gross
As an innovation this year Hebrew graduation has been changed
from Hanukah to the close of Passover, Three groups were gradu-
Sunday School Enrollment—292 boys, 342 girls, total 634.
The growth of our entire school is reflected in this department.
There is also better grading which necessitates a total of 29 classes,
Kindergarten — Enrollment: 41 boys, 29 girls, total 70.
We have an outstanding Kindergarten consisting of four classes,
Transportation
Constant planning under a special sub-committee has made pos¬
sible the servicing of all our membership even in the far outlying
Extra-Curricular Activities
Especially successful this past year has been the Sat. morning
Jr. and Pre-Jr. Congregation and Sunday morning Minyan Club.
Under the capable direction of Mrs. Philip Selber and Mrs. Bernard
Glossman, our Library facilities are used both for educational pur-
ated this year, two from the elementary department and one from
the advanced.

Youth Activities
Center Youth Organizations
Center Youth League—A co-ed USY Chapter for high school age, meeting bi-weekly with
varied programs, affiliated with national movement.
Charles Palot Club—USY Chapter similar to the above, with programs geared to Jr. High
school students, also part of national movement.
Boy Scouts Troop 14—For boys 11 to 14, meeting weekly.
Cub Scouts, Pack 14—Boys 8 to 11 participate in this organization, meeting weekly at
Den Mothers’ homes with monthly Pack meeings.
Girl Scouts, Troop 197—For girls who have outgrown the Brownies and wish to continue
Scouting.
Brownie Troops 28, 193, 210 & 346—A Scouting organization for younger girls with
weekly gatherings.
Major’s Pre-Teens—Social club, meeting monthly for 7th grade boys and girls.
Tween-Agers—Boys and girls of the 5th and 6th grades participate in this social club
meeting monthly.
Summer Day Camp
With school out our youngsters
look forward to our 8 week sum¬
mer program which is open to all
children from ‘yVz through 13. For
the 14 and 15 year old group, we
offer a Counselor-in-Training Pro¬
gram—with on the job experience.
This past summer 265 campers took
advantage of our summer activities.
Athletics
Teams in ping pong, softball, base¬
ball and basketball represented our
synagogue in city sponsored compe¬
tition during the 1960-61 season.
With the city table tennis tourna¬
ment being held in our Gymnasium,
interest in this activity increased
among our partcipants.
Weekly boys’ and men’s gym com¬
petition takes place year around with
basketball and weight h’^mg being
the most popular.

CENTER YOUTH LEAGUE OFFICERS
Seated: Eddie Mizrahi, Treasurer; Cynthia-Stillman, Rec. Secretary;

C Y L. Board
CHARLES PALOT CHAPTER OFFICERS
Seated: Madelyn Levin, Vice-President; Sheila Kramer, Recording
Secretary, Gail Price, Corres. Secretary; Judy Horovitz, Vice-Presi¬
dent.
Standing: Joel Selber, Advisor; Charles Slott, President; Jack Miz¬
rahi, Treasurer; Robert Fischer, Activities Director.
'
Center Youth League and Charles Palot Club
Southeast Region USY outstanding chapter awards were pre¬
sented to both of our USY chapters at the Tenth Annual Regional
Convention held in Jacksonville March 3-5, 1961. Hosted by the
local chapters, this Convention was praised as being the most success¬
ful to date.
Another highlight of the year was sending five delegates to the
National Convention in Chicago, thus giving Jacksonville the largest
delegation from our region. Mr. and Mrs. Bob Fischer accompanied
the group.
Outstanding CYL boy and girl members were announced at the
closing banquet with Evelyn Brauner and Marvin Slott taking the
honors.
Individual honors were capped by CYL’s Marvin Slott—S.E. Region
First Vice President, Eddie Mizrahi—Sub Regional Corresponding Sec¬
retary, CPC’s Bruce Yergin—Sub Regional Treasurer, and more than
half of the regional and sub regional chairmanships.
Our membership, programming, services, discussions and socials
have all made 1960-61 another banner. Both chapters had a member¬
ship of over 92 members with CPC reaching 104.
Our sincerest appreciation go to our advisors, Stanley Kantor, Ina
Selber and Joel Selber. Special thanks to our Youth Chairman Mrs.
Louis Slott and our Activities Director Bob Fischer.

Boy Scout Troop 14
DR. HARRY YERGIN
Scoutmaster
Scoutmaster . Dr. Harry Yergin
Asst. Scoutmasters .Nathan Weiss, Milton Berman and George Glossman
Chairman, Scout Comm Norman Moss
Scout Committee Harry Kolitz, Joe Mizrahi, Robert Fischer, Joseph P. Safer, Dr. Melvin
Néwman, Bernie Moskovitz, David Safer, Louis Slott* Dr. Harold Baker, Shelby Trager
day hike to Camp Blanding and two weeks at Camp Echokotee
where outstanding records were earned.
The joint Cub and Boy Scout Banquet was an outstanding
event of the year. Dr. Harry Yergin’s devoted leadership as Scout¬
master was recognized by presentation of a gift on behalf of the
parents. Presentation of the Troop charter of Ted Freedman took
place and the camping award certificate for outstanding camping
for 1960-61 was presented. Leon Yergin received the Bernard
Glossman Award for Scouting. A highlight of the evening was the
presentation of the President Eisenhower Award for achievement
and advancement with a congratulatory letter to the Troop, which
was one of three to receive this distinction in the Mohawk District.
Troop 14 can be truly proud of its activities and welcomes
boys of 11 years and over to join its ranks.
101
An outstanding record of accomplishment was attained this
year by Boy Scout Troop 14. Within the past two years a proud
record of 30 Ner Tamid award winners was achieved and the
troop was twice cited for the attainment of 98 merit badges.
Eagle Scout status was reached by Leon Yergin, Charles Moskovitz,
Michael Safer, David Steinfeld, Stephan Kolitz, Eddie Mizrahi and
Frank Moss which brings the total number of Eagle Scouts in our
Troop to 30. A first prize blue ribbon for camping and cooking
was received at the Annual Scout Exposition.
The following personal honors were awarded: Order of the
Arrow to Leon Yergin and Frank Moss, third Palm on Eagle rank
to Bruce Yergin, first Palm on Eagle rank to Stephan Kolitz.
Among the outstanding activities was the opening three day
Camporee in the fall at Ross Allen’s in Silver Springs, the three

recorded and the Committee is looking forward to even greater
heights in the years to come. A varied program was offered to the

meetings, monthly Pack gatherings and the new summer activity of
monthly functions.

Girl Scouts and Brownies
Chairman Mrs. Leon Schinasi
Brownie Troop 346 Mrs. Joe Fraden, Leader; Mrs. Gus Goldstein, Co-Leader
Brownie Troop 210 Mrs. Albert Lipschutz, Leader; Mrs. Max Kaplan and
Mrs. Louis Fraden, Co-Leaders

MRS. LEON SCHINASI
The Brownie Troops and Girl Scout Troops sponsored by the
Center Sisterhood show a great deal of growth in the number of
girls and leaders who now make up the troops. We now have 5
Brownie Troops and 2 Girl Scout Troops.
Scouting is a foundation for our youth which enables them to
grow into womanhood with the knowledge of getting along with
their neighbors and the customs and way of life of other children
around the world.
Our mothers give their time and knowledge to these groups of
children and help them have a full rewarding childhood.

CENTER DA Y CAMP
Camp Yom Tov
Staff: Director: Robert Fischer, Counselors:

Marvin Slott. Specialists: Mrs. M. J. Reiser,

With ever increasing popularity our summer program continues to grow as can be witnessed by our record breaking enrollment of
162 campers average per week and outstanding program. Among the new program additions were the following:
* In addition to our Midget Baseball Team—we now have a
team in the Tadpole City League.
* A C.I.T. program for boys and girls 14 and 15 years of age.
* Ice skating and archery for a new addition to our activities
list.
* Our Tadpole Team winning the city championship in their
first year of competition.
* Color War and the Annual Awards Night were the closing
highlights of the third successful summer under the direc¬
tion of our Activities Director Bob Fischer.

Mr. and Mrs. Club
President Mr. and Mrs. Jack Silverman
1st Vice-Pres Mr. and Mrs. Nathan Barber
2nd Vice-Pres Mr. and Mrs. Ted Friedman
Treasurer Mr. and Mrs. Sidney Polster
Recording Secretary Mr. and Mrs. Carl Miller
Corresponding Secretary Mr. and Mrs. I. Gartner
Board Members: Messrs, and Mesdames Harry Kohtz, Abe
Stein, Herbert Perlman, Ai Levintman, Leon Schinasi and
Lester Glasser.
The Mr. and Mrs. Club of Jacksonville is now in its eighth year.
It is one of the community’s outstanding social organizations. With
a membership of 100 or so couples, the Club attempts to create an
atmosphere of congenial fellowship among the Jewish couples of the
community.
Two social functions are held monthly, one is an informal
small affair, while the other is an elaborate dinner dance. All social
affairs are arranged by committees selected from the membership.
There are two outstanding affairs held during the year, one the New
Year's Eve Party and the other the Installation Banquet. Couples in¬
terested in affiliation are requested to contact any of the officers of
the organization.

Board of Directors: Messrs, and Mesdames: Eli Becker, Eddie
Bernard, Hymie Fialkow, Marvin Ganson, Irwin Gendzier, Joe
Goldman, Allie Goldstein, Larry Jaben, Hy Kliman, Robert Las¬
ris, Ben Miller, Sam Silver, Henry Weintraub and Harry Yergin.
Now in its fifth year, the Amity Club’s purpose is to bring
Jewish couples in the community together in a friendly and
sociable atmosphere.
Each month the club has an elaborate social affair «arranged by
committees selected from the membership. The highlights of the
year’s activities are the New Year’s Eve dinner-dance and the
June installation banquet. Husbands and wives, serving together,
govern the affairs of the organization. They also serve together
when working with a committee arranging a monthly affair.
The organization is open to married couples between the
ages of 32 and 47 with a maximum membership of 85 couples.
Couples interested in membership are requested to contact any of
the officers or members for details.

AL L. SCHNEIDER
Serving the entire Jewish community of Jacksonville, the Jewish
Community Council is the central Welfare Fund and Community
Relations body of the local Jewish community. An association of
individuals, synagogues, organizations and social service agencies, the
program encompases key activities which give unity, cohesion and
coordination to local Jewish communal life.
A community-wide annual Welfare Fund Campaign provides
support to significant local, national and overseas organizations. The
National United Jewish Appeal, has expressed American Jewry’s
responsibility for the welfare and rehabilitation needs of Jewish
communities the world over for over 20 years. River Garden He¬
brew Home for the Aged and the Jewish Welfare Society are the
local beneficiaries.
Specializing in
FORMAL TABLE COVERINGS

River Garden Hebrew Home For The Aged

Irving Klepper
River Garden having celebrated its 15th anniversary looks to
the future with high hopes and anticipation for future expansion of
its facilities. Increasing needs for the specialized services of the
Home makes it imperative to look ahead.
The Home is now overcrowded as it serves 78 people in a plant
designed for 70 residents. As many more people reach late muturity,
the need for the Home will increase proportionately.
The master plan is to replace the original wooden structure with
a modern 3 story fireproof building to accommodate 50 more resi¬
dents. It will be specifically designed for the ailing and infirm aged
who require nursing care. Another phase of the program is to pro¬
vide for short term convalescent care on a community wide basis
where the individual could come to the Home for post hospital
care and then return to his own home.
In addition, River Garden is considering a housing program ad¬
jacent to the Home to provide an independent living arrangement
for elderly people who could participate in various aspects of the
Home’s program. It would provide the aged with an easy access to
companionship, enriching experiences, as well as a sense of security.
Although River Garden’s plans for the future are ambitious,
they are the only solution to long range planning for anticipated
growth of the elderly segment of the population. River Garden has
proven its worth to the Community by its services to aged and infirm.
The Home again looks to all its friends to share and benefit in the
future growth of River Garden.
Judy's MATERNALANE

Ladies Hebrew Sheltering Aid Society
OFFICERS
President Mrs. Moe B. Safer
Vice-President Mrs. William Goldman
Vice-President Mrs. Roy J. Sloat
Recording Secretary Mrs. William Silverman
Financial Secretary Mrs. Sol Katz
Corresponding Secretary Mrs. Sam Lazarus
Treasurer, Dues Mrs. I. Zaslow
Treasurer, Projects Mrs. Louis Witten
Ladies Hebrew Sheltering Aid Society was founded thirty-eight
years ago as an aid society to help the needy of the Jewish faith.
It was always the dream and hope of our founders to have a Home
for the Aged in Jacksonville.
This dream became a reality when River Garden Hebrew Home
for the Aged was founded. Since that time, our role became that
of the auxiliary to the Home, helping to nurture its growth and
development.
Another successful year has been completed by Ladies Aid. Our
eight hundred members play a special part in serving River Garden
wherever we are needed and in many capacities. We provide extra-
budgetary needs such as furnishings, redecorating, air conditioning
and kitchen and infirmary equipment. Funds for these needs were
raised through Tombola, Donor and Social Printing projects as well
as through annual dues and Life Memberships in Ladies Aid. Linens
were provided through our annual Linen Shower.
In the realm of service, Ladies Aid sponsors activity and volun¬
teer programs in the Home. Since its inception four years ago, over
twenty-one thousand hours of service have been given to the resi¬
dents through our Gray Ladies program. We sponsor regularly
scheduled entertainment and parties through our Special Holidays
and Friendly Visitors program. River Garden residents participate
in projects served by volunteers of the auxiliary such as the resi¬
dents magazine, Ceramic Classes and the Hobby Shop.
This year from our Senior Citizens program has emerged a club
with its own officers. It is called "The Golden Sunshine Club."
Their goals are to create a new fellowship of senior citizens for the
purposes of companionship and to participate in civic and philan¬
thropic activities.

LEWIS MEISEL
Financed by Community Chest-United Fund, United Jewish Wel¬
fare Fund and the Levy Memorial Fund, the Jewish Welfare So¬
ciety is the Family and Children’s Case Work Agency of the Jewish
community. Last year it served 181 local families and individuals,
and 159 non-residents.
Its main purpose is to counsel families and individuals and to
provide resources to alleviate problems that threaten their well be¬
ing and to help prevent such problems. The scope of problems in¬
cludes parent-child, marital, emotional, economic, health, aging,
employment and conflict with law.
It also counsels and help maintain unmarried mothers through
their pregnancy and places children for adoption. It licenses, main¬
tains and supervises foster homes. It processes the applications and
provides the case work for the residents of the River Garden Hebrew
Home for the Aged. It does the resettlement work for the Jewish
Community and assists with immigration problems. It provides fi¬
nancial assistance to local families and assists transients, and pro¬
vides a limited program for prisoners at Raiford.
We are grateful for your response to our need for foster homes
and jobs and to Mrs. Sanders Tofield, who joined our staff in Octo¬
ber I960 and who so ably helped secure them for our clients. These
and the need for "Big Brothers” are pressing and continuous. If
you can help, please call us.

The Zionist Organization of America is the only overall Jewish
movement throughout the world which encompasses the totality of
Jewish life with Israel as the central point of gravity.
Before the establishment of the State, our main goal was a politi¬
cal one. Now the cultural phase of our program as well as public
relations, is the foremost task. We continue the struggle for the
revival of the Hebrew language wherever a Jew may live. The
Zionist idea permeates the overwhelming majority of Jews every¬
where . . . Regardless whether one has Orthodox, Conservative or
Reform religious affiliation, or perhaps none at all, he may never¬
theless be an ardent associate and supporter of the Zionist Move¬
ment.
The American Zionists have accomplished a fine and remarkable
record of good public relations between the American people and
the State of Israel.
The Z.O.A. welcomes all Jewish men to participate in its ac¬
tivities.

In Jacksonville roughly 830 women make up the ranks of
Senior Hadassah, which meets on the second Tuesday of every
month. These women are joined together by a common bond of
helping Hadassah in her program of health, vocational and edu¬
cational services to Israel, and of a desire to be an integral part
of American community life.
Hadassah is the world's largest Zionist organization, number¬
ing 1250 chapters containing a total of more than 318,000 mem¬
bers. Besides being active in Israeli social and medical welfare,
Hadassah members have also been instrumental in stimulating,
on the American scene, a greater interest and understanding of
the Jewish cultural heritage.
'''
n
Newly returned from Israel, Hadassah’s president, Mrs. Joel
Fleet, was most impressed with all facets of Israeli life. She
visited the Alice Seligsburg vocational and educational school,
which specializes in fashion and design; and of course the
Hadassah Medical Center which is considered to be a citadel of
science in the East, serving everyone regardless of race, color, or
creed.
Outstanding events of the coming year will include the open¬
ing luncheon, the Israeli Showcase, and the Membership Tea.
In the spring Hadassah’s donors will be honored and in May
will be the closing luncheon.
Hannah Senesch

Young Judaea
Jerry Linsey Marilyn Halpern
Hashomerim Zionettes
Linda Hilsenrad
Tova
Candy Cohn
Bas Yisroel
Brian Benwick
Blue and White Stars
Five Young Judaean Clubs totaling 85 members were active
in Jacksonville during 1960-61. The Zionette Club and Hashome¬
rim Club are senior clubs and Tova, Bas Yisroel, Blue and White
Stars are junior clubs.
The clubs follow the Young Judaean program dedicated to
Jewish cultural stimulation such as holiday celebrations, discus¬
sions, speakers, debates, love of Israel and Zionism, Israeli singing
and dancing, American affairs and participation in community
endeavors. Sociability of the members together with the clubs’
aims and purposes make for enjoyable and meaningful club ex¬
periences.
The clubs had outings, hikes, campfires, speakers, song fests,
parties and the combined affair of all the clubs, "The Israeli Fair"
held annually at River Garden. The funds raised at this affair are
sent to the Israeli Scouts to build and maintain the "Hava," a
meeting center for American Young Judaeans and the Tsofim in
Israel.
This year Young Judaea purchased their own camp in Hen¬
dersonville, N. C. with 18 attending from Jacksonville. This camp
is a non-profit undertaking of the Southern Zionist Youth Com¬
mission, of which Mrs. Harold Rabhan is local Chairman.

Irwin (Bud) Goldberg
1961 was a banner year for the A. B. Weil Lodge of B’nai
B’rith. Membership and activities increased and the lodge received
an award for outstanding service to the Duval County Sheriff’s
Office. A successful bowling league was formed, tours made of the
Naval Air Station and the County Jail as well as tremendous philan¬
thropies such as free medical care in our hospitals, Hillel foun¬
dations in colleges, the ADL, and scholarship loans.
The men of B nai B’rith are working together in the constant
struggle to make our community a better place to live.
B:'NAI B RITIÍ WOMEN
At the biennial B’nai B’rith Convention a telegram was received
from President Kennedy which read, "We depend on organizations
such as yours to assist us in carrying out our intent to bring relief to
a world wasted by hunger, disaster and tyranny.”

National Council
Jacksonville Section
President Mrs. Harry Slutzah
Vice-President Mrs. Arnold Vandroff
Vice-President Mrs. Gustav Dobbie
Vice-President Mrs. Sol Goldman
Vice-President Mrs. Joe Goldman
Corres. Secretary Mrs. Benjamin Benson
Auditor Dr. Lillian Mark
Treasurer Mrs. David Gradman
Recording Secretary Mrs. Lewis Ansbacher
Financial Secretary Mrs. Bernard Sonnenfield
The National Council of Jewish Women is a volunteer service
organization which through a program of service, education and
social action helps to meet human needs.
Council’s local activities include recreational activities at the
Juvenile Shelter and at River Garden Home for the Aged. In the
past two years it has tested the hearing of almost 20,000 Duval
County children, has continued to provide wheelchairs, crutches
and hospital beds through our Medical Loan Shelf and has sewed
layettes for needy families.
Council continues its support to Israel by training leaders for
of Jewish Women
welfare and educational work through scholarships and by sending
toys and clothing to children in Israel, and has built a new Hebrew
Univ. High School.
Through its programs and projects Council provides a strong,
unifying cord of mutual interests and aims for all Jewish women,
regardless of a diversity of thinking and background, so that they
may work together in the spirit of Judaism for the good of all hu¬
manity.
The Jacksonville Section has both a daytime and evening group,
meeting every third Wed. and fourth Thurs., respectively.

Brandéis University
National Women 's Committee
OFFICERS
President Mrs. Felix Glickstein
Vice-President (Annual Membership) Mrs. Sidney Geist
Vice-President (Life Membership) Mrs. Ike Witten
Vice-President (Book Fund) Mrs. Mel Newman
Recording Secretary Airs. Harold Newman
Corresponding Secretary Mrs. Donald Cohn
Financial Secretary Mrs. Herman Miller
Treasurer Mrs. Hyman Katz
Parliamentarian Mrs. Leonard Cohn
Brandéis University, the first Jewish-sponsored non-sectarian
school of higher learning, opened its doors in 1948. This year
marked the graduation of the 10th class at the Waltham, Massa¬
chusetts campus.
The Brandéis University National Women’s Committee was
founded with the sole purpose of the support of the University
Library, the heart of its cultural life. No fund-raising activities
are held, as all money is derived from Annual and Life Member¬
ships, and from contributions to the Book Fund. There are over
71,000 members in 113 Chapters throughout the nation.
The National Women’s Committee notes the development of
Brandéis University with pride. The growth of the Library must
keep pace with the growth of the University, and the addition of
Graduate Schools places new demands on the Library. Women
throughout the country have made a definite contribution not
only to the Library but also to the University by fostering good
will for Brandéis in their respective communities.

Progressive Credit Union
President Moe B. Safer
Vice-President Israel Lipson
Secretary Eugene L. Henry
Treasurer Joseph Cohen
Directors Max Rubin, Benjamin Fink, Louis Berinhout,
Edwin Presser
Asst. Directors Samuel Schuster, M. B. Glickstein,
Herman Gelman, Sanford M. Whitman
Credit Committee Samuel J. Klepper, Chairman; Louis Moss,
Harry Kline
Supervisory Committee Max Becker, Chairman; Isidore
Weiss, Herbert Sandler
The Progressive Credit Union sponsored by the Jewish Progres¬
sive Society and chartered by the State of Florida, has continued
to grow in its 22 years. The combined membership of Credit Unions
in the U.S. totals more than 10 million members and has over
four billion dollars in assets.
From a very modest beginning of 20 members and $500.00 in
assets, the Progressive Credit Union has grown to over 350 mem¬
bers and more than $200,000.00 in assets. It is organized to en¬
courage thrift and provide a safe, convenient medium whereby mem¬
bers can invest and accumulate savings. Being regularly examined
by the Comptroller of the State, the savings of members are safe¬
guarded in several ways.
Left to right: Israel Lipson, Moe B. Safer, Joseph Cohen and Eugene
L. Henry.
Life insurance equal to a member’s savings up to $1,000.00
and Loan Protection Insurance is paid by the Progressive Credit
Union without any cost to the individual member. The year’s ac¬
tivities climax with the annual meeting at the Center.
Last year the Board declared a 3% dividend on savings and
10% rebate of interest to borrowing members. A cordial invitation
is extended to anyone who wishes to join.

Jewish War Veterans
Meyer Leibovitz Post 199
Commander Elliot Starr
Sr. Vice-Commander Emanuel Fisher
Jr. Vice-Commander Max Rosenberg
Quartermaster Max Becker
Adjutant Leon Robins
Chaplain Rabbi Moshe V. Goldblum
Trustees Harry Blair, C. S. Schlein, Harry Mazey
ELLIOT STARR
The Jewish War Veterans of the United States is the oldest
veterans organization in America. Organized in 1896 under char¬
ter of the U. S. Congress, it has served with honor and distinction
throughout the United States and its possessions in the years since
its inception.
The aims and objectives of J.W.V. is allegiance to the Consti¬
tution of the United States of America, its flag and institutions; to
combat the forces of hate and bigotry and to defend the honor of
our people; to always practice good citizenship and loyalty to our
Country. To perpetuate the memory honor of our heroic men who
have made the supreme sacrifice. It also gives council and aid to
the veteran and his family in time of need.

Grey Oaks Lodge
President

MURRAY B. GLICKSTEIN
The Grey Oaks Lodge located at Switzerland in St. Johns Coun¬
ty was organized in 1955 by members of the Jacksonville Jewish
community as a social and recreational club with a limited member¬
ship. The club grounds are located on a picturesque wooded bluff
overlooking the widest expanse of the St. Johns River. In addition
to the rustic clubhouse and auxiliary buildings, the club has a
1,000 foot dock, an Olympic-sized swimming pool, bath house and
other facilities.
Although membership is limited, there remains an opportunity
for members of the community to join and share in the delightful
activities of this unusual recreational organization. Many new ac¬
tivities have been added this past year and the membership increased.

♦All days of the Jewish year begin at sundown. Therefore, each holiday
really begins at sundown of the day preceding our symbol.
1 | ROSH HASHANAH (the Jewish New Year), a solemn two-day
A holiday when the Shofar (ram’s horn) sounds forth in the
^ synagogue to call the people to repentance and to rewaken re¬
sponsibility to the Lord.
TZOM GEDALIAH (the Fast of Gedaliah), indicated here by
my the Hebrew characters for “tzom." On this day, Jews mourn
for Gedaliah, a Jew whom the conquering Babylonians un¬
expectedly appointed Governor of Palestine. The Jews saw
in this a sign that their nation would rise again, but Gedaliah was
ruthlessly assassinated.
YOM KIPPUR (Day of Atonement), holiest of the Jewish
L 4e I year. This is a time of solemn self-evaluation—-a time for
confession, repentance, and asking pardon of the Lord. Tradi¬
tion decrees, too, that on Yom Kippur, Jews forgive and ask forgiveness
of their fellowmen. On Yom Kippur Eve there is a service of great and
haunting beauty, which never fails to deeply stir those who hear it. As
the doors of the Ark are opened, the congregation stands—and is at once
caught up in a reverent and expectant hush. The sacred Torah scrolls
are taken out of the Ark, and held before the congregation. Then the
white-robed cantor begins the ancient chant, Kol Nidre (all vows), his
voice soaring and descending to an old, old melody which has become
famous throughout the world for its lucid poignancy. On the following
morning, the congregation gathers again in the synagogue, where they
remain, throughout this austere fast day, in continuous prayer. On
Yom Kippur, as on other holidays, the prayers are read from the Machzor
(holiday prayer book) which we show as our symbol, above.

m „
[.ftjf SUKKOT (the Feast of Tabernacles). The veritable Thanks-
giving commemorates the harvest in the Holy Land. It is
• ^ therefore symbolized by the Esrog and Lulav (citrus and palm
branch) with myrtle and willow twigs. Also on this happy festival, Jews
everywhere build Sukkot (temporary shelters)—often hung with fruit and
other decorations— to remind them that the Israelites lived in open huts
during their desert wanderings, after the Exodus from Egypt.
SHEMINI ATZERET (Eighth Day of the Festival). Because the
Jews were, and in many places still are, a pastoral and agri¬
cultural people, a short prayer for rain is said daily during
a large part of the year. But on Shemini Atzeret, a holiday of the har¬
vest season, the synagogue service gives particular prominence to special
prayers which ask for rain to bring full crops in the coming year—hence
our rain-drop symbol.
ÍÉ SIMHAT TORAH (literally, “Rejoicing in the Law") —On
this final day of the holiday season, the annual reading of
the Torah (Five Books of Moses) is completed and begun
anew. The sacred scrolls of the Torah are carried in joyful procession
around the synagogue. Children love this holiday because they take part
in the procession, carrying colorful flags, and receive gifts of sweets.

HANUKAH (Dedication)—Anniversary of the day when the
Jews, led by Judah Maccabee and his brothers, freed the
Temple from Syrian invaders. At the Temple’s rededication,
only enough undefiled oil was found to light the Holy Lamp for one day.
Miraculously, it burned eight days. Thus, on the first day of Hanukah,
Jews light one candle of the Menorah (eight-branched candlestick, our
symbol), adding another flame each day until all eight are lit.
ASARAH B’TEVET (Tenth Day of Tevet)—a day of fasting
(\ and mourning. It was on the 10th day of Tebeth that the
Babylonians began their siege of Jerusalem. Thereafter, the
walls were breached (hence our symbol: the wall-breaching catapult), the
city was taken, the Temple destroyed, and the first Hebrew Common¬
wealth brought to an end.

|| HAMISHA ASAR B’SHEVAT (Fifteenth Day of Shevat), the
New Year of the Trees. Often referred to as the Jewish
«L» Arbor Day, this holiday commemorates the ancient need for
trees in the Holy Land. It is celebrated everywhere with appropriate songs
and games. Fruits long associated with the trees that once grew in the
Holy Land (and have begun to grow there again) are eaten on this day,
as part of the celebration. In Israel, the day is also observed in a very
practical way. There, where the land had been denuded by centuries of
neglect, and the need for trees has re-arisen (and reforestation has be¬
come a national goal), children and adults plant trees throughout the
land.

25 ADAR II
fTAANIT ESTHER (Fast of Esther)—This holiday commemorates
the heroism of Esther, beautiful Jewish Queen of Persia.
Joined by her people, she fasted for three days before risking
her life by appearing unsummoned before her King, Ahasuerus. Her
pleas led to the salvation of the Jewish people and the downfall of
Haman (see Purim, below).
■ i» PURIM (literally, “lots”) — this gayest of Jewish holidays is
P ■ nonetheless named for the lots cast before Haman to select
I a day of execution for the Jews. On the fateful day, how¬
ever, it was the wicked Haman who perished—thanks to Queen Esther
and her cousin, Mordecai. The full story is told in the Megillah (Book
of Esther, our symbol) which, on this day, is read aloud twice in the
synagogue. During the readings, children twirl their gragefs (noise-
makers) whenever Haman is mentioned, to drown out his name.

PESAH (Passover), the Feast of Unleavened Bread—an eight-
day celebration of the deliverance of the Jews from Egyptian
slavery. During the traditional Seder meal, on the first two
nights of Passover, Jewish families read the Haggadah (a ritual recita¬
tion of the story of the Exodus). Throughout the Passover period, foods
are selected according to special dietary laws. Instead of bread, Jews
eat matzoh (unleavened bread) to recall the hasty flight from Egypt,
when there was no time to allow the bread to rise. (Hence, our matzoh
symbol.)

===== PAGE 213 =====
LAG BA’OMER (33rd Day of the Omer)—this holiday com¬
memorates the lifting of a plague from the students of
Rabbi Akiba, an event v/hich occurred on the 33rd day of
Counting the Omer (the counting of the “omer” or “measure” of new
grain begins on the Second Day of Passover, and continues for 49 days).
In observing Lag Ba’omer, Jews honor the memories of Rabbi Akiba and
his copatriots, Bar Kochba and Rabbi Bar Yachai. These leaders de¬
voted their lives to an heroic effort to end the Roman occupation and re¬
establish the Jewish nation. (Rabbi Akiba ultimately died by torture at
the hands of the Romans.) The holiday is celebrated with athletic games
(symbolized here by bow and arrow) because Rabbi Akiba and his
students carried bows and arrows to disguise their true purpose when
they went into the woods to pursue the forbidden study of the Torah.

m SHAVUOT—On this day, the Ten Commandments are read in
the synagogue to commemorate the giving of the Law (Torah)
to Moses on Mount Sinai. Shavuot also marks the early wheat
harvest in the Holy Land. On this holiday Jews decorate their homes and
synagogues with greens and flowers.
